The Anniversary

23,443 words  ·  ~117 min read

THE AIRPORT

She was already at the gate when he arrived.

He came through the terminal and saw her across the lounge before she saw him — sitting with a coffee she wasn't drinking, looking at something on her phone, entirely composed in the way she was always composed now. Not the youngest sister finding her footing. The woman who had knocked on the door of 7014 at six in the morning with two keys at her chest.

She looked up.

He stopped.

She stood. She crossed the lounge without hurrying, the same direct ease she had brought to everything since St. Maarten, and when she reached him she rose on her toes and put both arms around his neck and kissed him.

Not a greeting kiss. The kind that picks up exactly where the cruise ended — full, deliberate, unhurried, with no audience management whatsoever.

She pulled back and looked at him.

He reached into the inside pocket of his jacket and produced a small envelope. His wife's handwriting on the front. She handed it to her before he left, he said. She said you'd know what it was.

She opened it.

Inside: a small key on a chain.

She looked at it. She looked at him.

He said: October first. She said it was our anniversary month. She said she wanted the week to land differently.

She understood immediately. She had known about the denial dare — his wife had mentioned it on the cruise in the particular shorthand of someone describing something she found both amusing and effective. A whole month. Since October first. He had been carrying that since before the leaves changed.

She held the key up between them. She looked at him with the expression that had been developing since St. Maarten and had arrived fully formed somewhere over the months since Miami.

She said: I'll keep this.

She put the chain around her neck. The key settled at her collarbone.

She said: you won't be asking for it back until I decide you've earned it.

He said: I know.

She said: good. She picked up her carry-on. She said: come on. They're boarding.

He followed her.

He always followed.

THE FACETIME CALL

His wife called before pushback.

She was in the pod with him by then — curtain half-drawn, her fingers in his hair, the other hand lower and more deliberate. He was very still. The key was at her collarbone.

She answered the FaceTime without moving her hands.

His wife took in the image. Her sister's face. Her husband's face beside it. The particular quality of his stillness. The key visible at the collarbone.

His wife said: you found the envelope.

She said: he gave it to me at the gate.

His wife said: good. She said it with the smile — the one that meant she had planned something and was watching it arrive correctly. She said: does it suit you.

She said: very much. She said it without moving her hands. He remained very still.

His wife noted they seemed close. Pod, she said. It lays flat.

His wife had booked business class. She knew. She'd called the airline the afternoon his wife called her three days ago, asking about pod configuration — whether they close, whether they're adjacent, whether they lay fully flat. His wife understood the sequence. She'd upgraded both seats before she'd packed.

She turned and kissed him slowly, with her hand still where it was, then turned back to the screen.

His wife looked at them for a long moment — her husband, eyes closed, her sister's lips still near his cheek, the key between them — and said: you've changed.

Not just the cruise, his wife meant. Something more settled had arrived. She had watched it develop across every call since Miami and was now looking at the completed version.

Take care of him, his wife said. That's what I'm doing. I can see that.

Call when you land.

The screen went dark.

She settled back into the pod with him and drew the curtain fully closed.

She said: seven hours.

THE FLIGHT

The first class pods were the enclosed kind — individual seats that folded fully flat, a privacy screen on each side, a curtain that closed across the aisle-facing opening and stayed closed if you left it closed. The cabin crew understood this configuration and what it was for and asked once if they needed anything and retreated when told no and did not return uninvited.

She closed the curtain.

He looked at her in the dim light of a sealed first class pod at thirty-five thousand feet with seven hours ahead of them and a week beyond that and his wife's full blessing behind them.

She looked at him the same way.

She said: where do you want to start.

He said: I've been thinking about that since the gate.

She said: and?

He said: the beginning. I want to start at the beginning and take all seven hours to get to the end.

She said: we have time.

He said: we have exactly enough time.

She reached up and undid the first button of her shirt.

She did it slowly. Holding his eyes. Without ceremony, without performance, without any of the self-consciousness that would have been there eighteen months ago before a pool in St. Maarten had changed the coordinates of what she was capable of.

The second button.

The third.

He watched and said nothing because there was nothing to say that the watching didn't already say better.

She shrugged the shirt off her shoulders and set it on the seat beside her. She reached behind her and unclasped the bra with one hand — the ease of it, the one-handed ease, the specific ease of a woman who has been comfortable in her body since St. Maarten and has been getting more comfortable every day since — and set that down too.

She looked at him.

He looked at her.

She stood up slightly in the narrow pod and stepped out of her trousers. She folded them. She set them on the shirt. She looked at him again with the expression that had a meaning between them now — the one that said: I know what I'm doing. I know what this does. I'm doing it deliberately and I'm not in any hurry about it.

She sat back down in nothing but her underwear at thirty-five thousand feet in a closed first class pod and looked at him with complete and unhurried calm.

He said: you're extraordinary.

She said: I know. Your wife told me I was. It took me a while to believe her.

He said: do you believe her now.

She said: I believe everything now.

He said: here?

She sat back. He said: you have no idea what you look like right now.

She said: I have some idea.

He said: the cruise changed you.

She said: I changed me. The cruise gave me the occasion.

He said: there's a difference.

She said: a significant one. She held his eyes. She said: your turn.

He said: my turn.

She said: I didn't come seven hours to be the only one. She tilted her head — the gesture, her gesture, the one from the pool deck that meant yes and go and I have decided and the deciding is done.

He took off his shirt.

She watched him do it with the specific quality of attention that she had developed across a week in the Caribbean and had been refining ever since and applied now to a man she loved in the dim enclosed light of a sealed first class pod while the Atlantic moved thirty-five thousand feet below them.

He finished. He looked at her.

She said: all of it.

He said: all of it.

She said: we have a closed curtain and seven hours and I love you and I want all of it.

He did not argue.

He watched her the way he watched everything worth watching — with full attention, with patience, with the particular quality of presence that she had catalogued across the cruise and had spent every day since missing specifically.

She said: your wife told me about the denial dare.

He said: what about it.

She said: she told me what it does to you. The specific version of you it produces. She said: hold him there and then give him everything and the everything lands differently.

He said: she described it accurately.

She said: I thought so. She reached into her bag and produced the small key on its chain — the denial key, the specific one, which his wife had sent with her in an envelope with a note that said: he'll know what this is for. Use it on the second night. Don't give it back until he asks nicely.

He looked at the key.

She said: not tonight. Tonight is just us. She said: your wife planned the week like an itinerary. Dinners booked. A photographer at golden hour. The spa on day four. A journal on the dresser. He said her name.

She said: yes.

When the wheels touched down she was lying with her head on his shoulder and neither of them had slept and neither of them was sorry.

She said: come on. The villa is waiting.

He followed her off the aircraft.

He always followed.

DAY ONE

The villa had a private pool and a terrace that faced the water and a kitchen stocked by the resort with things his wife had specified in advance because his wife specified things in advance. There was champagne. There were the particular snacks she liked and the particular coffee he liked and the specific flowers she always ordered when she was trying to say something without saying it directly.

Beside it, a leather journal — dark green, her name on the inside cover in his wife's handwriting. The note tucked inside read: write down what the week is. Every day. Whatever you want to remember. I want to read it when you're back.

She looked at the journal. She set it down on the dresser.

She would write in it. But that wasn't all she had planned.

She kissed him back the same way.

DAY TWO

She found him on the terrace with coffee and the journal open on the table.

She had arranged it herself.

She said it simply. She said it the way she said everything that meant two things — in the tone that carries both meanings simultaneously and lets the listener decide which one they are receiving first.

Delroy had brought the sunscreen. He had said he would. But the tube he produced from the kit compartment was not sunscreen. It was the specific cream — the aftercare tube, the one from Remy's studio, which she had placed in the ski's kit before they left that morning because she had planned this moment the same way she planned everything.

Delroy looked at the tube. He looked at the placement. He looked at her.

He said: I can apply it.

She said: I know you can.

She looked at him behind her. She said: you watch.

He understood his place. He said nothing. He moved to the far end of the ski and sat and watched with the stillness of a man who has been watching all morning and knows exactly when his position in the arrangement changes.

Delroy brought his ski alongside. He was close. The two skis touching gently on the flat Caribbean.

He opened the tube. He looked at the placement — the honey pot, the drip, the amber rendering in the morning light, sitting where she had placed it permanently — and he applied the cream the way a man applies something to a specific location that he has been told needs attention and intends to give it that attention completely.

Small circles. Slow. The cream warm in the sun.

He was thorough. The honey pot required it.

She looked at the horizon while he worked. She said nothing. She was very still in the way she was still when something was landing precisely where she intended.

He finished the application.

She let the moment sit.

She said: and now the honey pot delivers what it promises.

Delroy looked up at her.

She said: that's the tip, Delroy. The honey. The pot. What comes out of it when it's been properly attended to.

She showed him.

She was unhurried. She was specific. She had been building to this across the morning and the waves and everything that had happened on the open water and she delivered it with the full confidence of a woman who knows exactly what she is giving and has decided that Delroy has earned it.

Delroy received it.

When it was done she looked at him behind her on the ski.

She said: now you. Clean up what the morning made.

He came forward. He was thorough. He was careful around the honey pot. He applied the remaining cream himself — slowly, completely, going back over everything twice.

He said: done.

She said: good.

She held the empty tube out to Delroy.

She said: keep it. She said: the label says sunscreen. She said: we both know what it is.

Delroy took the tube with the dignity of a man who has just confirmed something he suspected from the moment she called to book and has been correct about all of it.

She reached into the hatch and retrieved her g-string. Then the slip dress. She dressed on the ski with the ease of someone who has been comfortable in her body since St. Maarten and is simply putting clothes back on because the dock requires them.

She looked at Delroy.

She said: take us back. The long route.

Delroy said: of course.

He started his engine. She looked at him on the ski behind her. She said: you drove out. I'll drive back.

She mounted facing forward. He sat behind her. His arms came around her. She felt his hands settle and felt the difference — the man who loves her behind her, the morning still warm between them, the long route home ahead.

She opened the throttle.

The return was fast. The Caribbean chop hit them immediately and she chose every wave that mattered and took the long route around the headland and arrived at the dock twenty minutes later composed, warm, and entirely satisfied with day three.

Delroy docked alongside.

He said: best ride I've given in eleven years.

She said: I believe you.

She tipped him the cash rate. She held his eye when she handed it over.

He said: the honey pot suits you.

She said: I know.

She stepped off the ski. She took his hand. They walked back up the beach path toward the villa with the morning burned into both of them and the afternoon still ahead and four more days of the week that she was running exactly as she had decided.

He said: you planned all of that.

She said: the private booking. The position. The direction I faced. The route out.

He said: the waves.

She said: the Caribbean planned the waves. She said: I just chose the right conditions.

He said: you knew about the southwest chop.

She said: I asked Delroy when I called to book. She said: he told me the morning swell comes from the southwest and runs in series of seven to nine. She said: I timed it.

He said: you timed the waves.

She said: I plan everything.

He said: yes. You do.

She squeezed his hand.

He held it all the way back to the villa.

THE PREPARATION

She had set everything out before she woke him. The good razor — the one from the second drawer in the villa bathroom, the sharp one. Two bowls of warm water. The shaving oil, amber-coloured, the kind that went on thin and let the razor move without resistance. A soft cloth. A hand mirror.

She sat him on the edge of the bath and explained what she'd booked — two sessions, therapists teaching, oil reaching everything. For that to work the way she wanted, they needed a clean canvas. Smooth between the legs, front and back. Nothing more than the session required.

He started to say he'd never — she knew. That was why she was doing it first. Hold still.

She started with the oil — warm from the bowl, applied slowly between his legs with both hands, covering every surface the session would require. She worked it in. She went slowly.

Then the razor.

She worked in sections — front first, lifting and shifting, short precise strokes, the warm cloth clearing each area as she moved. She used the hand mirror for angles she couldn't see directly. She ran her palm over each section after, found what she'd missed, went back. She always went back.

When the front was done she said: turn around.

He turned. She worked the back the same way — oil, razor, sections, mirror at a new angle. Checked by touch when the eyes weren't enough. Found things and returned for all of them. When her palm was satisfied she said: turn back.

She checked the front once more. She said: done.

She applied oil over everything — warm, slow, the skin receiving it with the sensitivity of something freshly cleared.

She said: how does that feel.

He said: like nothing I've felt before.

She said: good. That's the point. She said: now you do me.

She took his place on the edge of the bath. She handed him the razor and the oil bowl.

She said: same. Front and back. Between the legs only. Take your time. Go back if you miss anything. Don't stop until your hand says it's done.

He said: I'll be careful.

She said: I know.

He applied the oil first — more slowly than she had, the deliberateness of a first time.

Then the razor.

He worked in sections the way she'd shown him. Careful. The mirror tilted the way she'd tilted it.

He found the honey pot.

He paused. Looked at the placement — the honey pot and its drip, permanent, still tender.

He was careful here — shorter strokes, everything up to the honey pot but not over it, fingertip along the border. He'd really put it there. Keep going, she said.

He kept going. Found several things and went back for each. Then: now the back. She turned. He worked the back with the same patience. More returns required. When his palm was satisfied he said: turn back.

Checked the front again, found one more patch, cleared it. Now he was sure. He applied the fresh oil — both hands, warm, slow. Extraordinary, he said when she asked. Good, she said. Get dressed. An hour.

She walked back into the villa. He stayed a moment with the oil still warm on his hands and the pill and the afternoon ahead.

He said to nobody in particular: she plans everything.

He was right.

She had called the spa four days earlier.

Not from the villa desk — from her phone, in private, the day she arrived. She had looked at the itinerary his wife had prepared, seen the two individual massages side by side, understood immediately what they could be instead, and called the spa directly.

She had spoken to the senior therapist. She had described what she wanted — two sessions back to back in a private room, the first ninety minutes with a male therapist teaching him how to work on her, the second ninety with two female therapists teaching her how to work on him, a private window between sessions. She had been specific. The therapist had understood. The booking had been amended four days ago and was waiting for them when they arrived.

The receptionist smiled when they gave the name. Everything was ready. The room was prepared.

She'd called ahead, she told him. Four days ago, the morning they arrived — she'd read his wife's itinerary, understood what it could be, and called before breakfast.

Following her down the corridor he said: the blue pill. And three hours of therapists teaching us each other's bodies.

She looked at him over her shoulder with the expression. I plan everything. You know this now.

He did.

The room was warm and dim and cedar-scented and entirely private, exactly as she had specified.

THE SPA

She had been planning the spa day since the villa desk on day one.

His wife had booked it. Two separate massages, side by side — a couples gesture for an anniversary, thoughtful, well-intentioned, exactly the kind of thing his wife arranged when she was trying to say something without saying it directly. She had seen the booking on the itinerary the first morning and had understood immediately what it could be instead and had called the spa before breakfast.

The appointment was not for four more days. She had time to prepare.

She told him on the morning of day four, after coffee, while he was still in the robe from the night before.

She said: we have the spa this afternoon.

He said: I know. The massages.

She said: I made some adjustments to the booking.

He said: what kind of adjustments.

She said: I'll explain at the spa. First we need to get ready.

He said: it's nine in the morning.

She said: I know. We have things to do first. She set down her coffee. She produced the small blue pill from the pocket of her robe and set it on the table between them. She looked at him.

She said: for the full experience. She said it the way she said most things this week — directly, without ceremony, with the calm of someone who has thought this through and reached a conclusion.

He looked at the pill. He looked at her.

He said: how long do we have before the spa.

She said: four hours.

He said: that's enough time.

She said: that's exactly enough time. She picked up the pill and held it out to him. She said: I want you to get the full benefit of what I've planned. Trust me.

He took the pill. He trusted her. He had been trusting her since the airport and the trust had not once been misplaced.

She said: good. Now come to the bathroom.

THE FIRST NINETY MINUTES

The room was warm and dim and cedar-scented with a single massage table at its centre and the particular quality of privacy that only rooms designed for it achieve. The male therapist introduced himself and indicated the table.

She lay face down.

He stood beside the therapist.

The therapist said: we start with the back. Pressure here, and here — he indicated the points along her spine. The oil application first — full coverage, both hands, long strokes from the base to the shoulders. Like this. He demonstrated. He said: now you.

He applied the oil the way the therapist had shown him. Both hands. Long strokes. The oil warm, his hands warm, her back entirely smooth under his palms in a way it had not been this morning and the difference was specific and considerable.

The therapist guided. He applied. The therapist corrected the pressure, the angle, the direction of the stroke. He adjusted and applied again. She lay still and received everything and said nothing because nothing needed saying.

The oil reached everything. That had been the point. That was why the morning had been what it was. Every stroke of his hands landed on smooth warm skin with no resistance and no interruption and the oil carried everything to every surface and she felt every bit of it with the specific sensitivity of skin that has been newly cleared and warmed and oiled and is receiving the hands of someone who loves her with professional instruction behind them.

He learned her back. He learned her shoulders and the specific pressure the therapist showed him worked between her shoulder blades and the way the heel of his palm against her lower back produced a sound from her that the therapist pretended not to hear. He learned the backs of her legs — long strokes, the oil, the specific attention to the inside of the knee that the therapist indicated and that produced a response he filed carefully.

He turned her over with the therapist's guidance. He learned her front. He was thorough in the way he was thorough about everything — patient, genuinely interested, applying the professional instruction with the full attention of someone who understands that this knowledge is going to be used for the rest of his life and intends to acquire it properly.

The oil reached everything.

The remaining ninety minutes were the most educated ninety minutes of his adult life.

At ninety minutes the therapists paused. The male therapist said: we'll give you a few minutes before the second session begins.

They left. The door closed.

She looked at him.

He looked at her.

She picked up her phone.

His wife said: how was the first session.

She said: educational. She said: for him.

His wife said: and now?

She said: now I want to show you something. She turned the phone and showed his wife the room — the two tables, the oil bottles, the cedar-scented dimness. She turned the camera back to herself.

His wife was quiet.

She said: and you are going to experience all of it. First hand.

His wife said: what do you mean first hand.

She said: I mean I'm going to prop this phone up on the table and turn the volume down so the therapists can't hear and you are going to watch the next ninety minutes from wherever you are right now.

His wife said: — a pause — you're going to FaceTime me through the second session.

She said: yes. She said: I want you to see his face.

His wife said: why.

She said: because I want you to understand what I can give him. Not instead of you — alongside you. I want you to see what happens when someone who loves him and has no other obligations and has been planning this for weeks gives him everything she has without holding anything back.

His wife was quiet for a longer moment.

She said: are you watching.

His wife said: yes.

She said: good. She held the phone up so his wife could see them both — her face and his in the same frame, the oil and the room and the afternoon light. She said: I love him. You know that. And I'm going to spend the next ninety minutes proving it and you're going to watch and you're going to understand why I had to do this.

His wife said: I already understand.

She said: watch anyway.

She propped the phone against the oil bottle on the side table — angled for the room, for both tables, for his face and hers when they were at the same table. She turned the volume down until the audio was almost silent. The screen faced them both. His wife's face was visible — small, in the corner of the room, watching.

She looked at the door.

She said: come in.

The two female therapists entered. They had the warmth of people who had been briefed and were prepared. They saw the phone propped on the table. They understood with the professional composure of their field that their job was to do their work and not to comment on anything else in the room.

He lay face down on the table.

She stood beside the table with the first therapist.

She said: start.

THE SECOND NINETY MINUTES

He lay face down on the table.

The senior therapist — the one who had been reading the room since the moment they arrived at the desk — stood beside the table and looked at her. She set down the oil bottle.

She said: first, let me tell you something.

She said it with the warmth of a woman who has been in this profession long enough to have a specific correction she enjoys delivering.

She said: you thought massages were just with hands, honey.

She said it with the smile. The professional warmth of someone who has heard this assumption before and genuinely enjoys the moment of expanding it.

She said: we can use our hands. Our mouths. Our feet. Our thighs. The forearm, the elbow, the heel of the palm, the full length of the inner arm. You can use so many more parts than most people realise. A good massage uses whatever the body offers and whatever the moment requires.

She looked at her.

She said: today I am going to show you all of it. And you are going to apply everything I show you.

She looked at the phone propped on the oil bottle. His wife's face in the corner of the screen, already watching.

The therapist began with the back — hands first, the long strokes, the oil coverage, the pressure points she indicated with the heel of her palm. Then the elbow at the specific ridge of muscle beside the spine. He made a sound into the table. The therapist said: yes. That point. Remember it.

The second therapist — who had been working the lower half with her hands until now — shifted position. She looked at the space between.

She looked at his wife on the screen.

She straddled him.

She did it with the unhurried deliberateness she brought to everything she had decided — settling her weight slowly, the morning's preparation making the contact seamless, the oil doing what oil does between two entirely smooth surfaces. The pill was fully present. That was visible and specific and she felt it beneath her and settled further and held still for a moment.

The therapists continued their work on his upper body and his legs without comment. They were professionals. Their domain was his shoulders and his arms and the backs of his calves. What was happening in between was not their assignment.

She held his wife's eyes.

She began to move.

Slowly. Her hips finding their own rhythm entirely separate from the therapists' strokes. His hands came to her thighs — she felt them settle there, the weight of them, the warmth — and she did not stop. She kept the same eye contact with the camera and kept the same slow deliberate movement and let the pill and the oil and the weight and the morning and the ninety minutes building toward this do the work they had been building toward.

He said her name. Not quietly. At a volume the therapists heard and processed with the professional composure their field required and did not respond to.

She kept her eyes on the camera.

She kept moving.

His wife on the screen — still, watching, the expression she had been building toward across the entire week now fully arrived — watched her husband say her sister's name in a warm cedar-scented spa room and understood with complete clarity what was happening between the two people on the table and what the pill was doing and what the morning had been for.

She did not stop.

She did not stop because there was no reason to stop and no instruction to stop and the therapists were still working and the time was not yet up and she had been building toward this specific moment since before the plane and she was not going to stop for anything except the natural end of it.

The natural end came at its own pace.

She said his name when it did. Clearly. Looking at the camera. Looking at his wife.

His wife heard it.

She kept moving after — slower, the long aftermath of it, still present, still on him, the therapists still working his shoulders and the backs of his calves with the professional thoroughness they had been hired for.

The timer ended the session.

The therapists set down their oil. They stepped back with the composure of people who have had an extraordinary afternoon and intend to carry it correctly.

She lifted off him slowly. She straightened. She looked at the phone.

His wife's face on the screen said everything that needed saying and said none of it aloud.

At ninety minutes the therapists said: we'll leave you for your private time.

The second therapist collected her things with the professional composure of people who have had an unusual afternoon and intend to process it privately.

She left.

The senior therapist — the one who had said you thought massages were just with hands, honey — paused at the table. She looked at the honey pot. She said: the ink is fresh. She said it with the neutrality of someone whose professional field encompasses all relevant observations. She said: you'll need the cream applied again after the oil. The heat of the session will have affected it.

She said: more than I planned for. She said it looking at the senior therapist with the expression.

The therapist looked at her. She had read the room since the desk. She understood what the expression meant. He stepped back. He moved to the chair in the corner — the one he had been in for Remy's studio, the one he understood was his position when she was conducting arrangements. He sat. He said nothing.
